The Petrol Dealers Association of Malaysia (PDAM) has urged the government to delay introducing the RON 95 petrol subsidy rationalisation plan until critical industry issues are resolved, reports FMT.
PDAM president Khairul Annuar Abdul Aziz said dealer margins, which are fixed under the automatic pricing mechanism (APM), had not been revised since 2019 and no longer reflected current operating costs. Additionally, he voiced concern over the merchant discount rate (MDR) imposed on card payments, which increases as fuel prices rise but leaves dealersâ€™ per-litre commissions untouched. 
â€œWithout a margin revision, small and medium-volume station operators will face losses from the very first day of the RON 95 subsidy rationalisation implementation. And as pump prices increase under the rationalisation, the MDR rises accordingly, but dealer commissions remain static, resulting in a further erosion of margins,â€ he said in a statement. PDAM represents over 4,000 petrol stations nationwide and had previously voiced similar concerns last year (Facebook post attached below).
            




  




            
            
The RON 95 petrol subsidy rationalisation plan is set to be implemented in the second half of 2025, as revealed by second finance minister Datuk Seri Amir Hamzah Azizan a few days ago. A few days before that, former economic minister Rafizi Ramli said the plan had been fully handed over to the finance ministry.

This article first appeared in Forum, The Edge Malaysia Weekly on June 2, 2025 - June 8, 2025When Prime Minister Datuk Seri Anwar Ibrahim was released from jail in September 2004 after winning an appeal at the Federal Court, he had a coterie of highly qualified young people around him who formed the bedrock of the â€œReformasiâ€ movement.

Operating from a bungalow in Section 16, Petaling Jaya, key figures such as Anwarâ€™s daughter Nurul Izzah, outgoing Minister of Natural Resources and Environmental Sustainability Nik Nazmi Nik Ahmad and Sim Sze Tsin, now the Member of Parliament for Bayan Baru, worked behind the scenes while Anwar carried out his daily routine of interviews, forums and political ceramah.

Parti Keadilan Rakyat (PKR) was a five-year-old party then and represented by only one MP, Anwarâ€™s wife, Datin Seri Wan Azizah Wan Ismail.

This circle of young people on a mission formed the backbone of PKRâ€™s machinery for the 12th general election (GE12) in 2008, when the Reformasi movement denied Barisan Nasional (BN) a two-thirds majority in Parliament. It was the first dent in the armour for the formidable BN, which had been in power since 1974.

By 2008, Datuk Seri Rafizi Ramli, who was with Petronas then, was already playing a significant role in the party.

In 1999, he was among those involved with the international Free Anwar Movement lobbying the parliament in the UK and was an appointed member of the PKR Central Leadership Council a few years later. In 2003. Rafizi returned to join Petronas, which he left in 2009 to become the chief executive to the office of the Selangor State Economic Adviser. The adviser was none other than Anwar.

In the run-up to GE12, PKRâ€™s young candidates caught the imagination of both old and young voters yearning for reforms. Nurul Izzah won in Lembah Pantai, an urban Malay seat, beating Wanita Umno chief Tan Sri Shahrizat Abdul Jalil, a political heavyweight. Branded by the media as â€œPuteri Reformasiâ€, Nurul Izzah became a rising star.

Both Sim and Nik Nazmi contested and won state seats in Penang and Selangor respectively â€” two states that fell to the Opposition.

In GE13, GE14 and GE15 (2013, 2018 and 2022 respectively), Rafizi, Nurul Izzah, Nik Nazmi, Sim and many new faces established themselves in PKR as agents of Reformasi. Their campaigns were based on issues, and they did not come with baggage such as the taint of corruption and money politics and did not touch on the politics of race and religion. These younger PKR leaders represented all that Umno was not.

Yet, the outcome of the party elections concluded last week showed that PKR itself needs to reform. Rafizi alleged that money politics had seeped into the party. And, to some extent, Nurul Izzahâ€™s statement on money laundering added credence to Rafiziâ€™s allegations.

â€œIf money politics were not rampant, there would be no reason for Nurul to raise the matter of ensuring it (money politics) does not become part of the culture in PKR,â€ says a divisional leader.

At the time of writing, Rafizi and Nik Nazmi had tendered their resignations from the Cabinet, though these had yet to be accepted. Technically, both were appointed by Anwar, and the party elections have nothing to do with their positions in government.

Nevertheless, whatever the outcome, the party elections conclude with PKR being split between two factions.

One faction, linked to Rafizi, is hell-bent on keeping the party away from Umno-style money politics. Among those alleged to have influenced the party elections are former MIC treasurer Datuk R Ramanan, who has vehemently denied the accusations. Ramanan is a newly elected vice-president and is poised for a full ministership.

The other faction, which opposes Rafizi, has sent two clear messages. The first is that he and his allies can be outmanoeuvred within PKR without breaking any rules; that he needs to sharpen his skills as a team player as he rises through the party ranks; and he must not underestimate the strength of other leaders.

The second, and equally important, message to the Rafizi faction is that Anwarâ€™s successor after 2028 â€” when Anwar can no longer serve as PKR president â€” is expected to be his daughter Nurul.

In the recent PKR elections, where the Rafizi faction was outclassed, the losing faction blamed the changes in the election voting system made in December last year. Previously, PKR used a â€œone-man, one-voteâ€ system, allowing every member to vote for the top six positions and 20-member council. The changes to the constitution that were passed during the December congress introduced a delegate system, however, giving branches with more members greater delegate representation at the national level. The Rafizi faction contends that this system was compromised by money politics at branch meetings, influencing delegate selection and the election outcome.

Another change in the party polls was the introduction of blockchain technology for e-voting, requiring members to complete an e-KYC process to be eligible. The Rafizi faction claims flaws in the e-voting system disadvantaged them. Their concerns are not without merit.

Before PKR demands â€œReformasiâ€ in the next general election, it must first clean up its own house and rid itself of money politics. The election results clearly suggest that something is amiss â€” especially when incumbents lose branch positions to newcomers. This may explain why Nurul has begun focusing on educating members about anti-money laundering laws.

Apart from reforming PKR, Nurul faces her biggest test leading the partyâ€™s seat negotiations ahead of the Sabah State Election. The negotiations will be tough because Gagasan Rakyat Sabah is a coalition of eight parties whereas Pakatan Harapan (PH) comprises four parties, including Sabahâ€™s United Progressive Kinabalu Organisation. If BN joins the talks, more than 14 parties will be vying for 73 state seats.

Meanwhile, PKR must also gear up for GE16, which is due by November 2027.

Since 2008, Rafizi has been key in shaping PKRâ€™s strategy, focusing on the partyâ€™s strengths. The party is weak in Malay-majority seats but has a fighting chance in mixed constituencies â€” areas also targeted by Umno and BN.

Given the partyâ€™s current state after the recent elections, PKR remains fragile. Uniting the party is the next major task for the untested Nurul.

As for Rafizi, his focus is unlikely to be on the next general election but his sights will be on the 2028 party elections and GE17, when Anwar will no longer be president. For the moment, Rafizi and Nik Nazmi have to play the long game. As Nik Nazmi said when resigning, quoting a British politician, â€œI left cabinet to focus more on politics and public serviceâ€.

For now, Rafizi,47, and his allies such as Nik Nazmi have to play the long game in politics and bide their time.

As Nik Nazmi aptly said, quoting the late British MP Tony Benn: â€œI am leaving the Cabinet to give more time to politics and public service.â€

From Terence Netto In spurning overtures to assuage his defeat in the PKR polls, former party deputy president Rafizi Ramli is treading a sensible path towards a return to prominence in his party. He has declined his successor Nurul Izzah Anwarâ€™s offer to be appointed as a second deputy president in the party. He has also said he is not leaving PKR to form a new party. Also, he has cautioned PKR against accepting Tengku Zafrul Aziz as a member, now that the latter has resigned from Umno and is seeking to join PKR. Rafizi said allowing Tengku Zafrul, who has not resigned from the Cabinet, to join PKR would set an unhealthy precedent. Tengku Zafrul joined the Cabinet on an Umno ticket. If he leaves Umno, he should properly resign from his ministerial post. By turning a blind eye to this flouting of rules, Prime Minister Anwar Ibrahim is weakening a rules-based system. By contrast, Rafizi is seen as adhering to the rules that underpin the system, whereas Anwar is seen as sticking by them only when it suits him. Combined with Anwarâ€™s tardiness in implementing the Reformasi agenda, it is he who is losing credibility, while Rafizi appears to be gaining it. Hence, it is better for Rafizi to tread the more sensible path of withdrawing from the fray following his defeat in the deputy president contest. A brief stay in the political wilderness is sometimes necessary for politicians to resurge when circumstances change and those who replaced them are found to be terribly wanting. Nurul Izzah is a candidate for this fate because she has done little to merit her elevation within PKR, her success at beating Rafizi coming when there has been an electoral wipeout of PKR in her familyâ€™s bastion of Permatang Pauh. All she has going for her is the aura of being Anwar Ibrahimâ€™s daughter. But this kind of political capital is the flimsiest of assets. When the beguiled finally discover it to be empty, they are apt to regard it as fraudulent in the first place. The reckoning that follows is often grim for aura-riding leaders, from Eva PerÃ³n to Imelda Marcos. Rafizi would do well to take note that political exile has often been the proving ground from which estimable leaders have returned stronger. What he could now beneficially do is formulate a political ideology for PKR, a party that has lacked one since the Reformasi movement was born 27 years ago. The Reformasi movement was always sure of what it was against â€“ cronyism, nepotism and abuse of power â€“ than what it was for and the philosophical basis of that alignment. It is easier to fight for principles than to live by them. The latter requires knowledge of political philosophy. But just now, Rafizi has shown he has principles. He now has the time to explore their philosophical underpinnings. Â  Terence Netto is a senior journalist and an FMT reader. The views expressed are those of the writer and do not necessarily reflect those of FMT.
